--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

What patterns characterize LOW Openness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Openness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Openness

#### Language Style
- **Simple Vocabulary**: The use of straightforward and common words (e.g., "good," "bad," "funny") indicates a preference for familiar language rather than complex or abstract terms.
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Many sentences are short and declarative, lacking variety in structure. This can be seen in phrases like "I don't know" and "I miss."
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing often flows in a disorganized manner, reflecting a lack of deep reflection or exploration of ideas (e.g., jumping from one topic to another without clear transitions).

#### Values
- **Preference for Familiarity**: There is a strong emphasis on comfort and routine, as seen in the frequent references to home, family, and familiar activities (e.g., missing home, discussing favorite foods).
- **Conventional Interests**: The writers express enjoyment in mainstream activities (e.g., reality TV, popular video games) rather than exploring niche or unconventional interests.
- **Focus on Relationships**: A significant concern for personal relationships, particularly romantic ones, is evident, indicating a value placed on social connections over exploration of new ideas or experiences.

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety About Change**: Writers express discomfort with new environments and experiences (e.g., adjusting to college life, feeling homesick), indicating a resistance to change.
- **Fear of Judgment**: There is a recurring concern about how others perceive them, particularly in social situations (e.g., worrying about trying too hard with girls).
- **Struggle with Self-Identity**: Writers often express uncertainty about their own feelings and identities, particularly in relation to others (e.g., questioning the nature of relationships).

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Avoidance of New Experiences**: The writers often mention a reluctance to engage in new activities or meet new people, preferring familiar routines and environments.
- **Social Withdrawal**: Expressions of loneliness and discomfort in social settings suggest a tendency to withdraw rather than seek out new connections.
- **Focus on Immediate Gratification**: There is a tendency to prioritize short-term pleasures (e.g., food, video games) over long-term goals or deeper exploration of interests.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynical or Critical**: A critical tone is often present, particularly when discussing new experiences or environments (e.g., negative comments about food or classes).
- **Frustration and Boredom**: Frequent expressions of frustration (e.g., with schoolwork or social situations) and boredom indicate a lack of enthusiasm for new challenges.
- **Nostalgia**: A longing for the past and familiar comforts is prevalent, suggesting a reluctance to embrace the present or future.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: Heavy use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a self-centered perspective, often reflecting personal concerns and experiences rather than broader ideas or collective experiences.

These patterns collectively illustrate a mindset characterized by resistance to change, a preference for the familiar, and a focus on immediate social and personal concerns, all of which are indicative of low levels of openness.
================================================================================

1. A writing assignment that lasts twenty minutes doesn't seem like a difficult task, but as I sit here I can remember all the papers that I have written. I usually sit and stare at my computer or take breaks to read what I have written before I continue. I can imagine that this paper will ramble and go into amazingly different subjects. Maybe I'm not so worried that I won't do well; I'm worried that it will be very hard and that I won't be able to pass the finals, which appear to be the biggest part of most grades. I've consulted the course schedule several times already to make sure I'm eligible for the class, and it appears that I am. Maybe I just shouldn't worry. I also worry that my professors will yell at me or tell me to leave the room. I'm basically a good student, and I usually don't disrupt the class. Sometimes, though, its hard to sit still and be quiet when you are sitting miles away from a professor and you are surrounded by other people that you are friends with. I guess its not really a big deal, but I don't like getting in trouble. What patterns characterize HIGH Openness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Openness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Openness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** The writing often reflects a free-flowing, unstructured thought process, with thoughts rapidly shifting from one topic to another (e.g., "I guess it's not really a big deal, but I don't like getting in trouble").
  - **Informal Tone:** Use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "whoa," "oh my god," "sucks") creates a conversational feel.
  - **Use of Questions:** Frequent rhetorical questions indicate curiosity and self-reflection (e.g., "Do people love me for me?").

- **Values:**
  - **Exploration and Curiosity:** A strong desire to explore new ideas, experiences, and possibilities (e.g., interest in different career paths, excitement about classes).
  - **Self-Improvement:** A focus on personal growth and achievement, often accompanied by high self-expectations (e.g., "I set really high standards for myself").
  - **Authenticity:** Concern about being true to oneself and how one is perceived by others (e.g., "I really want to make my parents proud").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Anxiety and Self-Doubt:** Frequent expressions of worry about performance, acceptance, and future prospects (e.g., "I feel like a loser," "I am worried that I won't be able to succeed").
  - **Social Dynamics:** Awareness of social relationships and their complexities, including feelings of homesickness and the impact of peer perceptions (e.g., "I feel kind of left out being so far away").
  - **Existential Questions:** Contemplation of identity and purpose, often questioning one's choices and future (e.g., "What can you really do with a BFA in Dance?").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Proactive Planning:** Engaging in behaviors like making lists and checking schedules, indicating a desire for organization amidst chaos (e.g., "I love buying planners").
  - **Social Engagement:** Interest in forming connections and understanding others, despite feelings of anxiety (e.g., wanting to meet new people, reflecting on roommate dynamics).
  - **Creative Interests:** Mention of artistic pursuits and imaginative thoughts (e.g., interest in animation, DJing, and dance).

- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **First-Person Perspective:** Predominantly using "I" and "my," indicating a focus on personal experiences and feelings.
  - **Inclusive Language:** Occasionally using "we" or "us" when discussing shared experiences, suggesting a desire for connection (e.g., "I guess most people enjoy some time to themselves").

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Openness writers exhibit a rich tapestry of thoughts characterized by curiosity, self-reflection, and a blend of anxiety and excitement about their experiences. Their language is informal and conversational, reflecting a stream of consciousness that reveals their inner workings and concerns about identity, achievement, and social dynamics.

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Openness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Openness

#### Language Style
- **Simple Vocabulary**: The use of straightforward and common words (e.g., "good," "bad," "funny") indicates a preference for familiar language rather than complex or abstract terms.
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Many sentences are short and declarative, lacking variety in structure. This can be seen in phrases like "I don't know" and "I miss."
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing often flows in a disorganized manner, reflecting a lack of deep reflection or exploration of ideas (e.g., jumping from one topic to another without clear transitions).

#### Values
- **Preference for Familiarity**: There is a strong emphasis on comfort and routine, as seen in the frequent references to home, family, and familiar activities (e.g., missing home, discussing favorite foods).
- **Conventional Interests**: The writers express enjoyment in mainstream activities (e.g., reality TV, popular video games) rather than exploring niche or unconventional interests.
- **Focus on Relationships**: A significant concern for personal relationships, particularly romantic ones, is evident, indicating a value placed on social connections over exploration of new ideas or experiences.

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety About Change**: Writers express discomfort with new environments and experiences (e.g., adjusting to college life, feeling homesick), indicating a resistance to change.
- **Fear of Judgment**: There is a recurring concern about how others perceive them, particularly in social situations (e.g., worrying about trying too hard with girls).
- **Struggle with Self-Identity**: Writers often express uncertainty about their own feelings and identities, particularly in relation to others (e.g., questioning the nature of relationships).

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Avoidance of New Experiences**: The writers often mention a reluctance to engage in new activities or meet new people, preferring familiar routines and environments.
- **Social Withdrawal**: Expressions of loneliness and discomfort in social settings suggest a tendency to withdraw rather than seek out new connections.
- **Focus on Immediate Gratification**: There is a tendency to prioritize short-term pleasures (e.g., food, video games) over long-term goals or deeper exploration of interests.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynical or Critical**: A critical tone is often present, particularly when discussing new experiences or environments (e.g., negative comments about food or classes).
- **Frustration and Boredom**: Frequent expressions of frustration (e.g., with schoolwork or social situations) and boredom indicate a lack of enthusiasm for new challenges.
- **Nostalgia**: A longing for the past and familiar comforts is prevalent, suggesting a reluctance to embrace the present or future.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: Heavy use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a self-centered perspective, often reflecting personal concerns and experiences rather than broader ideas or collective experiences.

These patterns collectively illustrate a mindset characterized by resistance to change, a preference for the familiar, and a focus on immediate social and personal concerns, all of which are indicative of low levels of openness.

- HIGH Openness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Openness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** The writing often reflects a free-flowing, unstructured thought process, with thoughts rapidly shifting from one topic to another (e.g., "I guess it's not really a big deal, but I don't like getting in trouble").
  - **Informal Tone:** Use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "whoa," "oh my god," "sucks") creates a conversational feel.
  - **Use of Questions:** Frequent rhetorical questions indicate curiosity and self-reflection (e.g., "Do people love me for me?").

- **Values:**
  - **Exploration and Curiosity:** A strong desire to explore new ideas, experiences, and possibilities (e.g., interest in different career paths, excitement about classes).
  - **Self-Improvement:** A focus on personal growth and achievement, often accompanied by high self-expectations (e.g., "I set really high standards for myself").
  - **Authenticity:** Concern about being true to oneself and how one is perceived by others (e.g., "I really want to make my parents proud").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Anxiety and Self-Doubt:** Frequent expressions of worry about performance, acceptance, and future prospects (e.g., "I feel like a loser," "I am worried that I won't be able to succeed").
  - **Social Dynamics:** Awareness of social relationships and their complexities, including feelings of homesickness and the impact of peer perceptions (e.g., "I feel kind of left out being so far away").
  - **Existential Questions:** Contemplation of identity and purpose, often questioning one's choices and future (e.g., "What can you really do with a BFA in Dance?").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Proactive Planning:** Engaging in behaviors like making lists and checking schedules, indicating a desire for organization amidst chaos (e.g., "I love buying planners").
  - **Social Engagement:** Interest in forming connections and understanding others, despite feelings of anxiety (e.g., wanting to meet new people, reflecting on roommate dynamics).
  - **Creative Interests:** Mention of artistic pursuits and imaginative thoughts (e.g., interest in animation, DJing, and dance).

- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **First-Person Perspective:** Predominantly using "I" and "my," indicating a focus on personal experiences and feelings.
  - **Inclusive Language:** Occasionally using "we" or "us" when discussing shared experiences, suggesting a desire for connection (e.g., "I guess most people enjoy some time to themselves").

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Openness writers exhibit a rich tapestry of thoughts characterized by curiosity, self-reflection, and a blend of anxiety and excitement about their experiences. Their language is informal and conversational, reflecting a stream of consciousness that reveals their inner workings and concerns about identity, achievement, and social dynamics.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Openness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Openness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. **Vocabulary Complexity**: Uses varied and abstract vocabulary vs uses simple and common words.  
2. **Sentence Structure**: Employs diverse and complex sentence structures vs relies on repetitive and short declarative sentences.  
3. **Thought Flow**: Exhibits a free-flowing stream of consciousness vs displays disorganized and abrupt topic shifts.  
4. **Interest in Exploration**: Shows enthusiasm for new experiences and ideas vs expresses reluctance to engage in unfamiliar activities.  
5. **Focus on Self-Improvement**: Prioritizes personal growth and high self-expectations vs emphasizes comfort and routine over self-development.  
6. **Social Engagement**: Actively seeks connections and reflects on social dynamics vs tends to withdraw from social situations and express loneliness.  
7. **Emotional Tone**: Balances anxiety with excitement about experiences vs often conveys frustration and nostalgia for the past.

What patterns characterize LOW Conscientiousness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Conscientiousness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Disorganized Structure**: The writing often lacks a clear structure, with thoughts presented in a stream-of-consciousness manner. Sentences are frequently fragmented or run-on, indicating a lack of focus (e.g., "I keep thinking 'compilation' for some reason...").
- **Repetitive and Rambling**: There is a tendency to repeat phrases and ideas, which can lead to a sense of aimlessness (e.g., "I wonder if...," "I hope that...").
- **Informal Tone**: The use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "Wow," "YAY!!") suggests a relaxed attitude towards the task at hand.

#### Values and Concerns
- **Lack of Goal Orientation**: Writers express uncertainty about assignments and a lack of clear objectives (e.g., "I'm not sure about how to do this assignment").
- **Procrastination and Distraction**: There is a notable tendency to get sidetracked by external stimuli (e.g., distractions from video games, music, or conversations).
- **Self-Doubt**: Frequent expressions of anxiety and insecurity about performance and abilities (e.g., "I hope that this isn't for a grade," "I don't really know what to write").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Impulsivity**: Decisions and thoughts appear impulsive, with little forethought (e.g., jumping from one topic to another without resolution).
- **Short Attention Span**: Writers indicate difficulty maintaining focus, often shifting topics rapidly (e.g., "I keep finding myself drawn into her conversation").
- **Avoidance of Responsibility**: There is a tendency to defer responsibility or express a lack of commitment to tasks (e.g., "I should really be thinking about school...").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Anxiety and Uncertainty**: A pervasive sense of anxiety about performance and future outcomes (e.g., "I guess I'm a little bit nervous about this").
- **Negativity and Self-Criticism**: Writers often express negative feelings about themselves and their abilities (e.g., "I feel bad right now," "I think I might be the one that has diabetes").
- **Lack of Motivation**: There is a general sense of lethargy or lack of drive to complete tasks (e.g., "I should really get started but I have no ideas").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: A high frequency of "I" and "my" indicates a self-focused perspective, often reflecting personal concerns and anxieties.
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: There is less emphasis on "we" or "us," suggesting a more individualistic approach rather than a collaborative or community-oriented mindset.

These patterns collectively illustrate a profile of low conscientiousness characterized by disorganization, lack of focus, anxiety, and impulsivity, which can hinder effective task completion and goal achievement.
================================================================================

- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to

- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

What patterns characterize HIGH Conscientiousness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Conscientiousness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Conscientiousness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Structured and Reflective:** The writers often engage in structured thought processes, reflecting on their feelings and experiences in a coherent manner. For example, they articulate their worries and concerns in a logical sequence, indicating a tendency to analyze situations deeply.
  - **Formal Vocabulary:** Use of more formal and precise vocabulary (e.g., "sacrifice," "monotonous," "convenient") suggests a focus on clarity and seriousness in communication.

- **Values:**
  - **Achievement-Oriented:** There is a strong emphasis on academic success and personal achievement. Writers express concerns about grades, assignments, and future educational opportunities (e.g., "doing well in all of my classes," "I must be the most ahead in my studies").
  - **Responsibility and Duty:** A sense of obligation to family and self is prevalent. Writers reflect on their parents' sacrifices and express a desire to honor those sacrifices through their own efforts (e.g., "I know that my Dad had to sacrifice...").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Fear of Failure:** A recurring theme is anxiety about not meeting expectations, both self-imposed and external (e.g., "I'm so scared that I am not going to do well here").
  - **Social Isolation:** Writers express feelings of loneliness and the struggle to connect with peers, indicating a concern for social acceptance and support (e.g., "I feel like I don't know anyone").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Proactive Planning:** Writers often mention their study habits and time management, indicating a tendency to plan ahead and prioritize responsibilities (e.g., "I feel like I should be studying instead of procrastinating").
  - **Emotional Regulation:** There is a tendency to suppress emotions or manage them internally, as seen in statements about hiding feelings to avoid vulnerability (e.g., "I tend to do that a lot. Hide my emotions").
  
- **Emotional Tone:**
  - **Anxious and Reflective:** The emotional tone is often anxious, with writers expressing worry about their performance and future. However, there is also a reflective quality, as they contemplate their feelings and experiences (e.g., "I guess I usually set mine aside in order to enjoy a small vacation from my life").
  
- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **First-Person Focus:** Frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a personal and introspective approach, emphasizing individual experiences and feelings.
  - **Collective References:** Occasionally, there are references to family and peers, suggesting a concern for how personal actions affect others (e.g., "I feel that the college experience is just as tough for them as for me").

These patterns collectively illustrate a conscientious personality characterized by a strong focus on achievement, responsibility, and emotional management, often accompanied by anxiety and a reflective mindset.
================================================================================

Summary so far:

- LOW Conscientiousness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Disorganized Structure**: The writing often lacks a clear structure, with thoughts presented in a stream-of-consciousness manner. Sentences are frequently fragmented or run-on, indicating a lack of focus (e.g., "I keep thinking 'compilation' for some reason...").
- **Repetitive and Rambling**: There is a tendency to repeat phrases and ideas, which can lead to a sense of aimlessness (e.g., "I wonder if...," "I hope that...").
- **Informal Tone**: The use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "Wow," "YAY!!") suggests a relaxed attitude towards the task at hand.

#### Values and Concerns
- **Lack of Goal Orientation**: Writers express uncertainty about assignments and a lack of clear objectives (e.g., "I'm not sure about how to do this assignment").
- **Procrastination and Distraction**: There is a notable tendency to get sidetracked by external stimuli (e.g., distractions from video games, music, or conversations).
- **Self-Doubt**: Frequent expressions of anxiety and insecurity about performance and abilities (e.g., "I hope that this isn't for a grade," "I don't really know what to write").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Impulsivity**: Decisions and thoughts appear impulsive, with little forethought (e.g., jumping from one topic to another without resolution).
- **Short Attention Span**: Writers indicate difficulty maintaining focus, often shifting topics rapidly (e.g., "I keep finding myself drawn into her conversation").
- **Avoidance of Responsibility**: There is a tendency to defer responsibility or express a lack of commitment to tasks (e.g., "I should really be thinking about school...").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Anxiety and Uncertainty**: A pervasive sense of anxiety about performance and future outcomes (e.g., "I guess I'm a little bit nervous about this").
- **Negativity and Self-Criticism**: Writers often express negative feelings about themselves and their abilities (e.g., "I feel bad right now," "I think I might be the one that has diabetes").
- **Lack of Motivation**: There is a general sense of lethargy or lack of drive to complete tasks (e.g., "I should really get started but I have no ideas").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: A high frequency of "I" and "my" indicates a self-focused perspective, often reflecting personal concerns and anxieties.
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: There is less emphasis on "we" or "us," suggesting a more individualistic approach rather than a collaborative or community-oriented mindset.

These patterns collectively illustrate a profile of low conscientiousness characterized by disorganization, lack of focus, anxiety, and impulsivity, which can hinder effective task completion and goal achievement.

- HIGH Conscientiousness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Conscientiousness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Structured and Reflective:** The writers often engage in structured thought processes, reflecting on their feelings and experiences in a coherent manner. For example, they articulate their worries and concerns in a logical sequence, indicating a tendency to analyze situations deeply.
  - **Formal Vocabulary:** Use of more formal and precise vocabulary (e.g., "sacrifice," "monotonous," "convenient") suggests a focus on clarity and seriousness in communication.

- **Values:**
  - **Achievement-Oriented:** There is a strong emphasis on academic success and personal achievement. Writers express concerns about grades, assignments, and future educational opportunities (e.g., "doing well in all of my classes," "I must be the most ahead in my studies").
  - **Responsibility and Duty:** A sense of obligation to family and self is prevalent. Writers reflect on their parents' sacrifices and express a desire to honor those sacrifices through their own efforts (e.g., "I know that my Dad had to sacrifice...").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Fear of Failure:** A recurring theme is anxiety about not meeting expectations, both self-imposed and external (e.g., "I'm so scared that I am not going to do well here").
  - **Social Isolation:** Writers express feelings of loneliness and the struggle to connect with peers, indicating a concern for social acceptance and support (e.g., "I feel like I don't know anyone").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Proactive Planning:** Writers often mention their study habits and time management, indicating a tendency to plan ahead and prioritize responsibilities (e.g., "I feel like I should be studying instead of procrastinating").
  - **Emotional Regulation:** There is a tendency to suppress emotions or manage them internally, as seen in statements about hiding feelings to avoid vulnerability (e.g., "I tend to do that a lot. Hide my emotions").
  
- **Emotional Tone:**
  - **Anxious and Reflective:** The emotional tone is often anxious, with writers expressing worry about their performance and future. However, there is also a reflective quality, as they contemplate their feelings and experiences (e.g., "I guess I usually set mine aside in order to enjoy a small vacation from my life").
  
- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **First-Person Focus:** Frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a personal and introspective approach, emphasizing individual experiences and feelings.
  - **Collective References:** Occasionally, there are references to family and peers, suggesting a concern for how personal actions affect others (e.g., "I feel that the college experience is just as tough for them as for me").

These patterns collectively illustrate a conscientious personality characterized by a strong focus on achievement, responsibility, and emotional management, often accompanied by anxiety and a reflective mindset.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Conscientiousness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Conscientiousness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. Language Structure: **Structured and coherent** vs **Disorganized and fragmented**  
2. Vocabulary Formality: **Formal and precise vocabulary** vs **Casual and colloquial language**  
3. Goal Orientation: **Achievement-focused and proactive** vs **Aimless and uncertain**  
4. Emotional Management: **Reflective and regulated emotions** vs **Anxious and self-critical**  
5. Responsibility Expression: **Sense of duty and obligation** vs **Avoidance of responsibility**  
6. Attention Span: **Focused and planned** vs **Easily distracted and impulsive**  
7. Social Concerns: **Awareness of social connections** vs **Feelings of isolation and disconnection**

What patterns characterize LOW Extraversion?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Extraversion writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Extraversion

#### Language Style
- **Simple and Direct Vocabulary**: The language tends to be straightforward, often focusing on immediate thoughts or feelings (e.g., "I'm hungry," "I don't like NYPD Blue").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many examples exhibit a free-flowing style, where thoughts are presented in a non-linear fashion, reflecting a focus on internal experiences rather than external interactions.
- **Repetitive Phrasing**: There is a tendency to repeat certain phrases or ideas, indicating a preoccupation with specific concerns (e.g., discomfort around others, feelings of being overwhelmed).

#### Values
- **Preference for Solitude**: A clear inclination towards solitude or small, familiar groups rather than large social gatherings (e.g., discomfort around boyfriend's friends).
- **Self-Reflection**: Writers often engage in introspection, analyzing their feelings and experiences, which suggests a value placed on self-awareness and personal growth.
- **Caution in Social Situations**: There is a recurring theme of anxiety or discomfort in social settings, indicating a value for safety and predictability over social engagement.

#### Concerns
- **Social Anxiety**: Frequent expressions of nervousness or discomfort in social situations (e.g., feeling scrutinized, not wanting to be in the spotlight).
- **Overwhelm and Stress**: Many writers express feelings of being overwhelmed by academic or social pressures, indicating a concern for mental well-being (e.g., "College is so stressful").
- **Fear of Judgment**: A notable concern about how others perceive them, leading to feelings of inadequacy or fear of disappointing others (e.g., worrying about a friend's visit).

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Avoidance of Social Interaction**: Indications of avoiding social situations or feeling uncomfortable in them (e.g., preferring to be part of the crowd, not wanting to engage with new people).
- **Focus on Personal Issues**: A tendency to dwell on personal problems or physical discomforts rather than engaging with others (e.g., discussing headaches, exhaustion).
- **Limited Engagement in New Experiences**: Hesitance to embrace new social opportunities or experiences, often reflecting a preference for familiar routines (e.g., feeling out of place during college parties).

#### Emotional Tone
- **Pessimistic or Anxious**: The emotional tone often leans towards anxiety, frustration, or pessimism, particularly regarding social interactions and academic pressures (e.g., "I feel like I have a heavy weight on my chest").
- **Self-Critical**: Writers frequently express self-doubt or negative self-perception, indicating a lack of confidence in social or academic settings (e.g., "I am such a horrible person").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: Predominantly using first-person pronouns ("I," "my") suggests a strong inward focus, emphasizing personal thoughts and feelings over collective experiences.
- **Limited Use of Second-Person Pronouns**: Rarely addressing others directly, which may indicate a preference for introspection rather than interaction.

These patterns collectively illustrate a profile of individuals with low extraversion, characterized by introspection, social anxiety, and a preference for solitude or familiar environments.

--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Extraversion

- **Language Style:**
  - **Conversational Tone:** The use of informal language and a conversational style is prevalent. For example, phrases like "Wow, I cannot believe..." and "I guess I need to give it more time" indicate a casual, relatable approach.
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** Many entries exhibit a free-flowing thought process, jumping between topics (e.g., workout plans, food preferences, and academic concerns), which reflects a high level of engagement with their surroundings.

- **Values:**
  - **Social Connection:** A strong emphasis on relationships and social interactions is evident. Writers express a desire to connect with friends and family, as seen in statements like "I am so ready to go home and see my family and friends."
  - **Community Involvement:** There is a recurring theme of wanting to engage with community or group activities, such as church involvement or team support, indicating a value placed on belonging and shared experiences.

- **Concerns:**
  - **Social Acceptance:** Writers frequently express worries about fitting in and making friends, as shown in phrases like "I hope I can get involved with the church" and "maybe I can become good friends with them."
  - **Academic Pressure:** Concerns about academic performance and workload are common, with expressions of anxiety about classes and grades, such as "I am kind of nervous about my classes."

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Proactive Engagement:** There is a tendency to seek out social opportunities and express excitement about future events (e.g., "I cannot wait" for a church retreat), indicating a proactive approach to socializing.
  - **Emotional Expression:** High levels of emotional expression are evident, with writers sharing feelings of excitement, nervousness, and longing, which reflects a willingness to be vulnerable and open about their emotional states.

- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **First-Person Plural and Singular:** Frequent use of "I" and "we" suggests a focus on personal experiences while also indicating a desire for shared experiences with others. This duality highlights both individual feelings and collective social aspirations.

- **Emotional Tone:**
  - **Optimistic Yet Anxious:** While there is an underlying optimism (e.g., "I hope that this year will get better for me"), there is also a notable anxiety about social and academic situations, reflecting a complex emotional landscape typical of high extraversion.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that thrives on social interaction, values community, and expresses a dynamic range of emotions, all while navigating the challenges of new environments and relationships.

6. Pronoun Usage: Frequent use of "we" alongside "I" for shared experiences vs. predominant use of first-person pronouns with limited second-person.

What patterns characterize LOW Agreeableness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Agreeableness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Direct and Blunt Vocabulary**: Use of straightforward language with little concern for politeness (e.g., "the cooks here should go down in the Guinness Book of World Records for being the first people ever to make chicken taste bad").
- **Sarcasm and Cynicism**: Frequent use of sarcasm (e.g., "I bet that they’re going to do this crap a couple times each semester") indicates a dismissive attitude toward authority or norms.
- **Complex Sentence Structures**: Some writers employ long, convoluted sentences that reflect a tendency to overthink or critique situations (e.g., philosophical musings about love and existence).

#### Values
- **Individualism**: A strong focus on personal beliefs and experiences, often at the expense of group harmony (e.g., criticism of liberal views on gun control).
- **Skepticism Toward Authority**: Distrust of institutions and norms, as seen in critiques of safety regulations and educational systems (e.g., questioning the effectiveness of gun laws).
- **Self-Interest**: Concerns primarily revolve around personal experiences and frustrations rather than collective well-being (e.g., complaints about food quality and roommate dynamics).

#### Concerns
- **Frustration with Social Interactions**: Expresses dissatisfaction with social dynamics, often feeling misunderstood or disconnected from others (e.g., struggles with roommate relationships).
- **Anxiety and Insecurity**: Frequent references to personal insecurities, such as confidence issues in social situations (e.g., wishing for more confidence to talk to girls).
- **Existential Doubts**: Engages in deep questioning of life’s meaning and personal significance, often leading to feelings of depression or futility (e.g., pondering the insignificance of humanity).

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Isolationist Tendencies**: Preference for solitude or limited social interaction, as indicated by reluctance to engage with roommates or peers (e.g., not spending time with roommates).
- **Critical Attitude**: A tendency to critique others and situations rather than seek understanding or compromise (e.g., negative comments about the food and living conditions).
- **Impulsivity and Distraction**: Frequent shifts in focus, such as jumping from one thought to another (e.g., from failing a quiz to observing squirrels), indicating a lack of sustained engagement with tasks or emotions.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynical and Pessimistic**: A general tone of skepticism and negativity, often highlighting frustrations and disappointments (e.g., lamenting the loss of freedoms and expressing dissatisfaction with college life).
- **Self-Deprecating Humor**: Use of humor to mask deeper insecurities or frustrations (e.g., joking about failing a quiz or the absurdity of life).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: A strong focus on the self, indicating a self-centered perspective (e.g., "I wish," "I think," "I feel").
- **Limited Use of Inclusive Language**: Rarely includes "we" or "us," suggesting a lack of connection to a broader community or group identity.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that is characterized by low agreeableness, marked by a critical, individualistic, and often cynical worldview.



What patterns characterize HIGH Agreeableness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Agreeableness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Agreeableness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Conversational Tone:** The writers use a casual, informal style, often resembling spoken language (e.g., "oh well," "gosh," "man").
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** There is a tendency for free-flowing thoughts, indicating openness and a lack of self-censorship (e.g., "I guess that my twenty minutes is up").
  - **Use of Exclamations and Interjections:** Frequent use of exclamatory phrases (e.g., "thank God," "woah!") conveys enthusiasm and emotional engagement.

- **Values:**
  - **Empathy and Concern for Others:** Expressions of care for friends and family are prevalent (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I hope things will get better for her").
  - **Supportive Attitude:** Writers often mention helping others or being there for them (e.g., "I go for moral support and to help out").
  - **Reflection on Relationships:** There is a focus on the importance of relationships and communication (e.g., "I wish I could tell him how much I love him").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Worry About Others' Feelings:** Writers express concern about how their actions affect others (e.g., "I always feel bad for the customers").
  - **Self-Reflection on Personal Growth:** There is a desire for self-improvement and learning (e.g., "I’m very open to learning if someone would teach me").
  - **Anxiety About Social Situations:** Some writers express nervousness about social interactions or performance (e.g., "I’m gonna have to walk down there and it will take forever").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Planning and Organization:** Writers often mention planning their time and activities (e.g., "I always try to plan out my day").
  - **Social Engagement:** Frequent references to social activities and interactions with friends (e.g., "I want one of our good friends from Chicago to come").
  - **Emotional Expression:** Writers openly share their feelings, whether positive or negative (e.g., "I am so sad that he had to pass on").

- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **Inclusive Language:** Use of first-person plural pronouns ("we," "us") suggests a collective mindset and a focus on group dynamics (e.g., "we could plot out our room").
  - **Frequent Use of "I":** Indicates personal reflection and self-awareness, often tied to emotional experiences (e.g., "I just want to tell him how important he is to me").

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Agreeableness writers exhibit a warm, empathetic, and socially engaged style. Their language reflects a strong concern for others, a desire for connection, and a tendency to reflect on their relationships and personal growth. The emotional tone is often positive, with a focus on support and understanding, which is evident in their choice of vocabulary and sentence structure.

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Agreeableness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Direct and Blunt Vocabulary**: Use of straightforward language with little concern for politeness (e.g., "the cooks here should go down in the Guinness Book of World Records for being the first people ever to make chicken taste bad").
- **Sarcasm and Cynicism**: Frequent use of sarcasm (e.g., "I bet that they’re going to do this crap a couple times each semester") indicates a dismissive attitude toward authority or norms.
- **Complex Sentence Structures**: Some writers employ long, convoluted sentences that reflect a tendency to overthink or critique situations (e.g., philosophical musings about love and existence).

#### Values
- **Individualism**: A strong focus on personal beliefs and experiences, often at the expense of group harmony (e.g., criticism of liberal views on gun control).
- **Skepticism Toward Authority**: Distrust of institutions and norms, as seen in critiques of safety regulations and educational systems (e.g., questioning the effectiveness of gun laws).
- **Self-Interest**: Concerns primarily revolve around personal experiences and frustrations rather than collective well-being (e.g., complaints about food quality and roommate dynamics).

#### Concerns
- **Frustration with Social Interactions**: Expresses dissatisfaction with social dynamics, often feeling misunderstood or disconnected from others (e.g., struggles with roommate relationships).
- **Anxiety and Insecurity**: Frequent references to personal insecurities, such as confidence issues in social situations (e.g., wishing for more confidence to talk to girls).
- **Existential Doubts**: Engages in deep questioning of life’s meaning and personal significance, often leading to feelings of depression or futility (e.g., pondering the insignificance of humanity).

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Isolationist Tendencies**: Preference for solitude or limited social interaction, as indicated by reluctance to engage with roommates or peers (e.g., not spending time with roommates).
- **Critical Attitude**: A tendency to critique others and situations rather than seek understanding or compromise (e.g., negative comments about the food and living conditions).
- **Impulsivity and Distraction**: Frequent shifts in focus, such as jumping from one thought to another (e.g., from failing a quiz to observing squirrels), indicating a lack of sustained engagement with tasks or emotions.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynical and Pessimistic**: A general tone of skepticism and negativity, often highlighting frustrations and disappointments (e.g., lamenting the loss of freedoms and expressing dissatisfaction with college life).
- **Self-Deprecating Humor**: Use of humor to mask deeper insecurities or frustrations (e.g., joking about failing a quiz or the absurdity of life).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: A strong focus on the self, indicating a self-centered perspective (e.g., "I wish," "I think," "I feel").
- **Limited Use of Inclusive Language**: Rarely includes "we" or "us," suggesting a lack of connection to a broader community or group identity.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that is characterized by low agreeableness, marked by a critical, individualistic, and often cynical worldview.

- HIGH Agreeableness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Agreeableness

- **Language Style:**
  - **Conversational Tone:** The writers use a casual, informal style, often resembling spoken language (e.g., "oh well," "gosh," "man").
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** There is a tendency for free-flowing thoughts, indicating openness and a lack of self-censorship (e.g., "I guess that my twenty minutes is up").
  - **Use of Exclamations and Interjections:** Frequent use of exclamatory phrases (e.g., "thank God," "woah!") conveys enthusiasm and emotional engagement.

- **Values:**
  - **Empathy and Concern for Others:** Expressions of care for friends and family are prevalent (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I hope things will get better for her").
  - **Supportive Attitude:** Writers often mention helping others or being there for them (e.g., "I go for moral support and to help out").
  - **Reflection on Relationships:** There is a focus on the importance of relationships and communication (e.g., "I wish I could tell him how much I love him").

- **Concerns:**
  - **Worry About Others' Feelings:** Writers express concern about how their actions affect others (e.g., "I always feel bad for the customers").
  - **Self-Reflection on Personal Growth:** There is a desire for self-improvement and learning (e.g., "I’m very open to learning if someone would teach me").
  - **Anxiety About Social Situations:** Some writers express nervousness about social interactions or performance (e.g., "I’m gonna have to walk down there and it will take forever").

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Planning and Organization:** Writers often mention planning their time and activities (e.g., "I always try to plan out my day").
  - **Social Engagement:** Frequent references to social activities and interactions with friends (e.g., "I want one of our good friends from Chicago to come").
  - **Emotional Expression:** Writers openly share their feelings, whether positive or negative (e.g., "I am so sad that he had to pass on").

- **Pronoun Usage:**
  - **Inclusive Language:** Use of first-person plural pronouns ("we," "us") suggests a collective mindset and a focus on group dynamics (e.g., "we could plot out our room").
  - **Frequent Use of "I":** Indicates personal reflection and self-awareness, often tied to emotional experiences (e.g., "I just want to tell him how important he is to me").

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Agreeableness writers exhibit a warm, empathetic, and socially engaged style. Their language reflects a strong concern for others, a desire for connection, and a tendency to reflect on their relationships and personal growth. The emotional tone is often positive, with a focus on support and understanding, which is evident in their choice of vocabulary and sentence structure.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Agreeableness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Agreeableness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. Language Tone: Casual and conversational style vs direct and blunt vocabulary.  
2. Emotional Engagement: Frequent use of exclamations and interjections vs cynical and pessimistic tone.  
3. Focus on Others: Expressions of care and concern for others vs self-centered perspective with limited empathy.  
4. Social Interaction: References to social activities and engagement vs isolationist tendencies and reluctance to connect.  
5. Reflective Attitude: Emphasis on personal growth and learning vs existential doubts and frustrations.  
6. Pronoun Usage: Inclusive language with "we" and "us" vs frequent use of first-person pronouns.  
7. Supportive Behavior: Mention of helping others and providing support vs critical attitude and complaints about situations.

What patterns characterize LOW Neuroticism?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Neuroticism writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Optimistic Tone**: The writers often express a positive outlook on life, focusing on the potential for good outcomes (e.g., "there is a reason and there is a plan").
- **Conversational and Casual**: The use of informal language and a conversational style (e.g., "oh well," "I guess") indicates comfort and ease in expressing thoughts.
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many entries reflect a free-flowing thought process, with ideas and topics transitioning smoothly (e.g., jumping from personal reflections to social events).

#### Values
- **Faith and Spirituality**: A strong emphasis on faith and its role in guiding actions and decisions (e.g., references to God, prayer, and biblical figures).
- **Friendship and Connection**: High value placed on relationships and companionship, often highlighting the importance of friendships (e.g., "I feel so blessed to have the friendship of Jeff").
- **Self-Reflection and Growth**: Writers express a desire for personal development and understanding, often contemplating their actions and beliefs (e.g., questioning their choices regarding alcohol).

#### Concerns
- **Future Orientation**: Writers frequently ponder their future, including career aspirations and personal relationships, but do so with a sense of hope (e.g., imagining a "fairy tale" life).
- **Social Dynamics**: Concerns about social interactions and relationships are present, but they are approached with a sense of humor or lightness (e.g., discussing crushes and friendships without excessive anxiety).
- **Acceptance of Change**: There is a recognition that life may not go as planned, but this is accepted with a positive attitude (e.g., "things happen differently than planned").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Decision-Making**: Writers demonstrate a tendency to make conscious choices that align with their values (e.g., choosing not to drink at parties).
- **Social Engagement**: Active participation in social events and a willingness to connect with others (e.g., attending parties, engaging in group activities).
- **Self-Control and Boundaries**: Indications of self-discipline, particularly in personal relationships and lifestyle choices (e.g., commitment to not engaging in sexual activity until marriage).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Inclusive Language**: Frequent use of "we" and "us" suggests a sense of community and shared experiences (e.g., "we all hung out").
- **Personal Reflection**: Use of "I" indicates a focus on personal thoughts and feelings, but often in a balanced way that does not dwell on negativity.

### Summary
Overall, the writers exhibit a low level of neuroticism through their optimistic language, strong values centered on faith and relationships, a proactive approach to concerns, and a balanced emotional tone. They reflect on their experiences with a sense of humor and acceptance, indicating resilience and a positive outlook on life.
================================================================================

What patterns characterize HIGH Neuroticism?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Neuroticism writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Frequent use of disjointed thoughts and rapid topic shifts, indicating a racing mind (e.g., "Hey! A castle! Groovy!").
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Many sentences begin with "I" or "I want," emphasizing a self-focused narrative (e.g., "I really want a new modem," "I want to join some clubs").
- **Informal Tone**: Casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "gosh," "oh well," "super expensive") suggest a conversational style that may mask underlying anxiety.

#### Values
- **Desire for Control**: A strong emphasis on wanting to manage various aspects of life (e.g., "I need to buy my Chemistry book," "I want to get into medical school").
- **Social Connection**: Frequent references to relationships and social dynamics (e.g., concerns about friends' feelings, wanting to meet new people), indicating a value placed on interpersonal relationships.
- **Achievement Orientation**: High concern for academic performance and future prospects (e.g., "I want to do well," "I am worried about this college algebra class").

#### Concerns
- **Health and Well-being**: Expressions of physical discomfort and mental fatigue (e.g., "I woke up feeling sort of sick," "I feel so awful that I cry").
- **Fear of Failure**: Anxiety about academic performance and social situations (e.g., "What if I fail," "I am scared to walk on the strip by myself").
- **Overwhelm**: Feelings of being burdened by responsibilities and commitments (e.g., "I am committing to too many activities," "There is so much to do during the week").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination and Avoidance**: Indications of delaying tasks or feeling overwhelmed by them (e.g., "I should do some reading for my classes tomorrow," "I want to go to the learning center for my calculus homework").
- **Social Comparison**: Frequent comparisons to others, often leading to self-doubt (e.g., "The guy sitting next to me is typing super fast," "I think she could get her feelings hurt").
- **Emotional Volatility**: Expressions of fluctuating emotions, from excitement to anxiety (e.g., "I am excited for the football game," "I feel I am not fully prepared").

### Summary
Overall, individuals exhibiting high neuroticism demonstrate a language style characterized by stream of consciousness and self-referential statements, values centered around control and social connection, concerns about health and performance, and behavioral indicators of procrastination and emotional volatility. These patterns reflect a heightened sensitivity to stress and a tendency to ruminate on personal and social issues.
================================================================================

- **Self-Reflection and Growth**: Writers express a desire for personal development and understanding, often contemplating their actions and beliefs (e.g., questioning their choices regarding alcohol).

#### Concerns
- **Future Orientation**: Writers frequently ponder their future, including career aspirations and personal relationships, but do so with a sense of hope (e.g., imagining a "fairy tale" life).
- **Social Dynamics**: Concerns about social interactions and relationships are present, but they are approached with a sense of humor or lightness (e.g., discussing crushes and friendships without excessive anxiety).
- **Acceptance of Change**: There is a recognition that life may not go as planned, but this is accepted with a positive attitude (e.g., "things happen differently than planned").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Decision-Making**: Writers demonstrate a tendency to make conscious choices that align with their values (e.g., choosing not to drink at parties).
- **Social Engagement**: Active participation in social events and a willingness to connect with others (e.g., attending parties, engaging in group activities).
- **Self-Control and Boundaries**: Indications of self-discipline, particularly in personal relationships and lifestyle choices (e.g., commitment to not engaging in sexual activity until marriage).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Inclusive Language**: Frequent use of "we" and "us" suggests a sense of community and shared experiences (e.g., "we all hung out").
- **Personal Reflection**: Use of "I" indicates a focus on personal thoughts and feelings, but often in a balanced way that does not dwell on negativity.

### Summary
Overall, the writers exhibit a low level of neuroticism through their optimistic language, strong values centered on faith and relationships, a proactive approach to concerns, and a balanced emotional tone. They reflect on their experiences with a sense of humor and acceptance, indicating resilience and a positive outlook on life.

- HIGH Neuroticism patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Frequent use of disjointed thoughts and rapid topic shifts, indicating a racing mind (e.g., "Hey! A castle! Groovy!").
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Many sentences begin with "I" or "I want," emphasizing a self-focused narrative (e.g., "I really want a new modem," "I want to join some clubs").
- **Informal Tone**: Casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "gosh," "oh well," "super expensive") suggest a conversational style that may mask underlying anxiety.

#### Values
- **Desire for Control**: A strong emphasis on wanting to manage various aspects of life (e.g., "I need to buy my Chemistry book," "I want to get into medical school").
- **Social Connection**: Frequent references to relationships and social dynamics (e.g., concerns about friends' feelings, wanting to meet new people), indicating a value placed on interpersonal relationships.
- **Achievement Orientation**: High concern for academic performance and future prospects (e.g., "I want to do well," "I am worried about this college algebra class").

#### Concerns
- **Health and Well-being**: Expressions of physical discomfort and mental fatigue (e.g., "I woke up feeling sort of sick," "I feel so awful that I cry").
- **Fear of Failure**: Anxiety about academic performance and social situations (e.g., "What if I fail," "I am scared to walk on the strip by myself").
- **Overwhelm**: Feelings of being burdened by responsibilities and commitments (e.g., "I am committing to too many activities," "There is so much to do during the week").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination and Avoidance**: Indications of delaying tasks or feeling overwhelmed by them (e.g., "I should do some reading for my classes tomorrow," "I want to go to the learning center for my calculus homework").
- **Social Comparison**: Frequent comparisons to others, often leading to self-doubt (e.g., "The guy sitting next to me is typing super fast," "I think she could get her feelings hurt").
- **Emotional Volatility**: Expressions of fluctuating emotions, from excitement to anxiety (e.g., "I am excited for the football game," "I feel I am not fully prepared").

### Summary
Overall, individuals exhibiting high neuroticism demonstrate a language style characterized by stream of consciousness and self-referential statements, values centered around control and social connection, concerns about health and performance, and behavioral indicators of procrastination and emotional volatility. These patterns reflect a heightened sensitivity to stress and a tendency to ruminate on personal and social issues.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Neuroticism, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Neuroticism when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. **Language Tone**: An anxious or uncertain tone vs an optimistic and positive tone.  
2. **Sentence Structure**: Frequent self-referential sentences starting with "I" vs varied sentence structures with inclusive language.  
3. **Concern Focus**: Preoccupation with health and performance vs a hopeful outlook on future possibilities.  
4. **Social Dynamics**: Anxiety about social interactions and comparisons vs light-hearted engagement in social relationships.  
5. **Emotional Expression**: Fluctuating emotions and feelings of overwhelm vs a balanced emotional tone with humor.  
6. **Proactive Behavior**: Procrastination and avoidance of tasks vs proactive decision-making and commitment to values.  
7. **Reflection Style**: Disjointed thoughts and rapid topic shifts vs smooth transitions and coherent reflections.

--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

What patterns characterize LOW Openness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Openness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Openness

#### Language Style
- **Simple Vocabulary**: The use of straightforward, everyday language with minimal complexity (e.g., "I don't know," "I miss home").
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Frequent use of short, declarative sentences that convey straightforward thoughts without elaboration (e.g., "I miss my dog," "I need a break").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: A tendency to write in a free-flowing manner without much organization, often jumping between topics (e.g., discussing homework, then shifting to personal feelings).

#### Values
- **Preference for Familiarity**: A strong inclination towards familiar topics and experiences, such as home, family, and established relationships (e.g., missing home, friends, and pets).
- **Conformity to Norms**: Expressions of discomfort with new experiences or environments, indicating a desire for stability and predictability (e.g., discomfort in new social settings, reluctance to engage with unfamiliar people).
- **Pragmatism**: Focus on practical concerns, such as schoolwork and daily routines, rather than abstract ideas or creative pursuits (e.g., discussions about homework, class schedules).

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety about Change**: Frequent expressions of homesickness and discomfort with new environments, indicating a struggle to adapt (e.g., "I am having a tough time adjusting").
- **Social Anxiety**: Concerns about social interactions and fitting in, often leading to feelings of loneliness or isolation (e.g., "I find myself being very uncomfortable").
- **Fear of Judgment**: Worry about how others perceive them, particularly in social situations (e.g., "I hope people don't think they are losers").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination**: A tendency to delay tasks, often leading to last-minute efforts (e.g., "I can't believe I waited until three hours before the due date").
- **Avoidance of New Experiences**: Reluctance to engage in new activities or meet new people, often preferring to stick with known routines (e.g., "I don't drink therefore being with a complete different crowd of people").
- **Focus on Routine**: A strong emphasis on daily routines and responsibilities, often expressing a desire for structure (e.g., "I have a schedule of events for the day").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynicism and Skepticism**: A tendency to express doubt or negativity about new experiences or ideas (e.g., "I think I feel incompetent when it comes to writing").
- **Nostalgia**: Frequent references to past experiences and relationships, indicating a longing for the familiar (e.g., "I miss my friends and family").
- **Frustration**: Expressions of annoyance or dissatisfaction with current situations, often related to social dynamics or academic pressures (e.g., "I dislike my roommate").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: Predominantly using first-person pronouns ("I," "my") to express personal feelings and experiences, indicating a self-centered perspective (e.g., "I am really enjoying myself").
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: Rarely using "we" or "us," which may suggest a lack of connection with others or a focus on individual experiences rather than group dynamics.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that is more comfortable with the familiar and resistant to change, reflecting the characteristics associated with low levels of openness.
================================================================================

qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that

- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

It just seems like we are ready to talk to some one we have never met before and never will see again more than we would talk to some one in a new class. People are overly concerned with making good impressions. It is important to realize that we are all pron to error. I say this, but I still worry about making good first impressions. I worry about it more if I think I am going to see the person again. If I don't think we will ever meet again, I am more relaxed. My mind is currently switching between a rapid pace of thoughts to blanking out. It is really scarry to think that your mind can just go blank when your not really doing any thing. I can understand your mind blanking out on a particular subject while you are trying to consentration on it, but the total lack of thought is just wierd. Oh my gosh. I just looked at the clock. Every one always seems to know what they are doing and seems to be in great shape. Of course, if you go to work out enough, you are going to be in shape. My back really hurts. I need to sit strait. I am always slouching over. I try to keep my back strait; it just never works for too long. I'm getting tired now. Typing is becoming harder as I become sleepier. Sleep is such an important part of my life. I probably sleep way too much, but it is better than some habbits people have. There is a consceted statement. Trying to say that I am better than some other people. Who do I think I am?  We all have are assests and faults. What time is it?  I really need to go soon. I just hope that when I get there I don't smell too bad or say my thoughts out loud. I am not saying them out loud, but typing them is very similar. The toughts must be processed to make sense and be comunicated. Writing is just a different form of comunication compared to talking. I hope I can find this place. I wish I had a friend to go with me. Large social events can be intemidating when every one seems to already know eachother. May be some of the people I met Saturday night will be there. Then I can finally figure out two of their names. It is so weird. I can remember the conversation we had, what they wore, where we were sitting, every thing but their names. I can't ask them because they remember mine. I don't want to hurt their feelings. I have to go now so I can get there on time. 

8. I have lots of things to today. I need to read and take note for all my classes. The bird is really getting on my nerves. I hope I will be able to finish all of my homework in time. If I write my paper at home I can print it at my sister's house, but I would have to finish it before I go home because I do not want to do it this weekend. I would ask someone to print it for me but I do not have enough time to go in the morning. I wonder what the psychology test is going to be like. I can't wait to go home. Once as I turn it on it will never be turned off and I need to study. I really enjoy my days off. Too bad this is the last time I get to sleep late and not have to go anywhere. 9. I suppose that the first sensation that comes to mind is contentment because Janet Jackson is playing on the radio. I love Janet Jackson. I would rather have ten friends that I know and trust than have a hundred that I barely know. I hate the concept of accquaintances. What patterns characterize HIGH Openness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Openness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Openness

#### Language Style
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing often reflects a free-flowing, unstructured thought process, with thoughts rapidly shifting from one topic to another (e.g., "I guess it's not really a big deal, but I don't like getting in trouble").
- **Informal Tone**: Use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "whoa," "oh my god," "sucks") indicates a relaxed and personal style.
- **Descriptive Vocabulary**: Frequent use of vivid adjectives and adverbs (e.g., "amazingly different subjects," "really happy," "totally secure") to express feelings and thoughts.

#### Values
- **Curiosity and Exploration**: A strong interest in new experiences and ideas is evident (e.g., excitement about classes, desire to meet new people).
- **Self-Reflection**: Writers often engage in introspection, questioning their feelings and motivations (e.g., "Do people love me for me?").
- **Value of Relationships**: Emphasis on the importance of friendships and family, often reflecting a desire for deep connections (e.g., "Without my ten friends, I would be lost").

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety and Self-Doubt**: Frequent expressions of worry about academic performance, social acceptance, and personal identity (e.g., "I am worried that I won't be able to succeed," "I feel kind of left out being so far away").
- **Fear of Rejection**: A recurring theme of anxiety related to social interactions and acceptance (e.g., "Rejection sucks," "I feel like a loser").
- **Desire for Authenticity**: Concerns about how they are perceived by others and a longing to be understood (e.g., "I wish that people would just understand people more and hate them less").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Planning**: Writers often mention making lists or plans to manage their tasks and responsibilities (e.g., "I love buying planners because I always write down my assignments").
- **Social Engagement**: A tendency to seek out social interactions, despite feelings of anxiety (e.g., wanting to meet new people, inviting friends over).
- **Creative Interests**: Expressions of interest in artistic or creative pursuits (e.g., wanting to animate Disney movies, excitement about DJing).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Perspective**: Predominantly using "I" and "my," indicating a focus on personal experiences and feelings.
- **Inclusive Language**: Occasionally using "we" or "us" when discussing broader social themes, suggesting a desire for connection with others.

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Openness writers exhibit a rich tapestry of thoughts and emotions characterized by curiosity, self-reflection, and a desire for connection, all conveyed through a casual and descriptive writing style. Their concerns often revolve around social acceptance and personal identity, while their behaviors reflect a proactive approach to managing their lives and relationships.
================================================================================
