--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Openness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Openness

#### Language Style
- **Simple Vocabulary**: The use of straightforward, everyday language with minimal complexity (e.g., "I don't know," "I miss home").
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Frequent use of short, declarative sentences that convey straightforward thoughts without elaboration (e.g., "I miss my dog," "I need a break").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: A tendency to write in a free-flowing manner without much organization, often jumping between topics (e.g., discussing homework, then shifting to personal feelings).

#### Values
- **Preference for Familiarity**: A strong inclination towards familiar topics and experiences, such as home, family, and established relationships (e.g., missing home, friends, and pets).
- **Conformity to Norms**: Expressions of discomfort with new experiences or environments, indicating a desire for stability and predictability (e.g., discomfort in new social settings, reluctance to engage with unfamiliar people).
- **Pragmatism**: Focus on practical concerns, such as schoolwork and daily routines, rather than abstract ideas or creative pursuits (e.g., discussions about homework, class schedules).

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety about Change**: Frequent expressions of homesickness and discomfort with new environments, indicating a struggle to adapt (e.g., "I am having a tough time adjusting").
- **Social Anxiety**: Concerns about social interactions and fitting in, often leading to feelings of loneliness or isolation (e.g., "I find myself being very uncomfortable").
- **Fear of Judgment**: Worry about how others perceive them, particularly in social situations (e.g., "I hope people don't think they are losers").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination**: A tendency to delay tasks, often leading to last-minute efforts (e.g., "I can't believe I waited until three hours before the due date").
- **Avoidance of New Experiences**: Reluctance to engage in new activities or meet new people, often preferring to stick with known routines (e.g., "I don't drink therefore being with a complete different crowd of people").
- **Focus on Routine**: A strong emphasis on daily routines and responsibilities, often expressing a desire for structure (e.g., "I have a schedule of events for the day").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynicism and Skepticism**: A tendency to express doubt or negativity about new experiences or ideas (e.g., "I think I feel incompetent when it comes to writing").
- **Nostalgia**: Frequent references to past experiences and relationships, indicating a longing for the familiar (e.g., "I miss my friends and family").
- **Frustration**: Expressions of annoyance or dissatisfaction with current situations, often related to social dynamics or academic pressures (e.g., "I dislike my roommate").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: Predominantly using first-person pronouns ("I," "my") to express personal feelings and experiences, indicating a self-centered perspective (e.g., "I am really enjoying myself").
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: Rarely using "we" or "us," which may suggest a lack of connection with others or a focus on individual experiences rather than group dynamics.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that is more comfortable with the familiar and resistant to change, reflecting the characteristics associated with low levels of openness.

- HIGH Openness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Openness

#### Language Style
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing often reflects a free-flowing, unstructured thought process, with thoughts rapidly shifting from one topic to another (e.g., "I guess it's not really a big deal, but I don't like getting in trouble").
- **Informal Tone**: Use of casual language and colloquialisms (e.g., "whoa," "oh my god," "sucks") indicates a relaxed and personal style.
- **Descriptive Vocabulary**: Frequent use of vivid adjectives and adverbs (e.g., "amazingly different subjects," "really happy," "totally secure") to express feelings and thoughts.

#### Values
- **Curiosity and Exploration**: A strong interest in new experiences and ideas is evident (e.g., excitement about classes, desire to meet new people).
- **Self-Reflection**: Writers often engage in introspection, questioning their feelings and motivations (e.g., "Do people love me for me?").
- **Value of Relationships**: Emphasis on the importance of friendships and family, often reflecting a desire for deep connections (e.g., "Without my ten friends, I would be lost").

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety and Self-Doubt**: Frequent expressions of worry about academic performance, social acceptance, and personal identity (e.g., "I am worried that I won't be able to succeed," "I feel kind of left out being so far away").
- **Fear of Rejection**: A recurring theme of anxiety related to social interactions and acceptance (e.g., "Rejection sucks," "I feel like a loser").
- **Desire for Authenticity**: Concerns about how they are perceived by others and a longing to be understood (e.g., "I wish that people would just understand people more and hate them less").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Planning**: Writers often mention making lists or plans to manage their tasks and responsibilities (e.g., "I love buying planners because I always write down my assignments").
- **Social Engagement**: A tendency to seek out social interactions, despite feelings of anxiety (e.g., wanting to meet new people, inviting friends over).
- **Creative Interests**: Expressions of interest in artistic or creative pursuits (e.g., wanting to animate Disney movies, excitement about DJing).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Perspective**: Predominantly using "I" and "my," indicating a focus on personal experiences and feelings.
- **Inclusive Language**: Occasionally using "we" or "us" when discussing broader social themes, suggesting a desire for connection with others.

### Summary
Overall, the HIGH Openness writers exhibit a rich tapestry of thoughts and emotions characterized by curiosity, self-reflection, and a desire for connection, all conveyed through a casual and descriptive writing style. Their concerns often revolve around social acceptance and personal identity, while their behaviors reflect a proactive approach to managing their lives and relationships.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Openness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Openness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. **Vocabulary Complexity**: Uses vivid adjectives and adverbs vs uses simple, everyday language.  
2. **Sentence Structure**: Employs varied and descriptive sentence forms vs relies on short, repetitive declarative sentences.  
3. **Topic Engagement**: Shows curiosity about new experiences and ideas vs prefers familiar topics and routines.  
4. **Social Interaction**: Actively seeks social engagement despite anxiety vs exhibits reluctance to engage with new people.  
5. **Self-Reflection**: Engages in introspection and questions personal feelings vs focuses on straightforward expressions of personal experiences.  
6. **Planning Behavior**: Frequently makes proactive plans and lists vs tends to procrastinate and avoid new tasks.  
7. **Emotional Tone**: Expresses excitement and openness to change vs conveys cynicism and discomfort with new experiences.

What patterns characterize LOW Conscientiousness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Conscientiousness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Informal and Disorganized**: The writing often lacks structure, with run-on sentences and frequent digressions (e.g., "I keep thinking 'compilation' for some reason...").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many examples exhibit a free-flowing thought process, jumping from one topic to another without clear transitions (e.g., "I wonder if I could be a genius writer. probably not. hmmm don't know what to write.").
- **Repetitive and Hesitant Language**: Frequent use of filler words and phrases (e.g., "I don't know," "I guess," "I wonder") indicates uncertainty and a lack of decisiveness.

#### Values and Concerns
- **Procrastination and Avoidance**: Writers express anxiety about assignments and deadlines but often delay action (e.g., "I should really be thinking about school...").
- **Low Self-Efficacy**: There is a recurring theme of self-doubt and questioning one's abilities (e.g., "I don't think I could change my mind about something...").
- **Focus on Immediate Gratification**: Interests in distractions (e.g., video games, music) often take precedence over academic responsibilities (e.g., "I got distracted and started watching him play for a minute.").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Impulsivity**: Decisions appear to be made on a whim, often without consideration of consequences (e.g., skipping responsibilities to play a show).
- **Lack of Planning**: Writers express a desire for change but do not articulate concrete steps to achieve their goals (e.g., "I need a change in my life, like college isn't a big enough change...").
- **Social Comparisons**: There is a tendency to compare oneself unfavorably to others, indicating a lack of confidence (e.g., "I am going to have like a couple of paragraphs. That is really sad.").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Anxiety and Frustration**: Many entries convey feelings of stress and overwhelm, particularly regarding academic pressures (e.g., "I am really having trouble recording my natural thought process...").
- **Discontent and Restlessness**: Writers often express dissatisfaction with their current situations, whether academic or personal (e.g., "I don't even feel at home here...").
- **Nostalgia and Regret**: There are reflections on past experiences that evoke a sense of longing or disappointment (e.g., reminiscing about family or past relationships).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The use of "I" is prevalent, indicating a self-focused perspective that often centers on personal feelings and experiences (e.g., "I feel bad right now...").
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: There is less emphasis on group identity or collaboration, suggesting a more individualistic approach to challenges.

These patterns collectively illustrate a tendency towards disorganization, impulsivity, and a lack of proactive behavior, which are characteristic of low conscientiousness.
================================================================================

--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

What patterns characterize HIGH Conscientiousness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Conscientiousness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Structured and Reflective**: The writing often displays a clear structure, with coherent thoughts and logical progression. For example, the writers frequently outline their concerns and resolutions in a methodical manner.
- **Formal Vocabulary**: Use of precise language and terminology related to academic and personal goals (e.g., "assignments," "education," "success").
- **Complex Sentences**: Many sentences are longer and more complex, indicating a tendency to elaborate on thoughts and feelings, as seen in the detailed descriptions of their academic and personal lives.

#### Values
- **Achievement-Oriented**: A strong emphasis on academic success and future aspirations (e.g., "I hope to continue my studies here and then go on to Law school"). This reflects a high value placed on education and career goals.
- **Responsibility**: Writers express a sense of duty towards their studies and family, often mentioning the sacrifices made by parents and the desire to honor those sacrifices (e.g., "I’m trying not to abuse any things they had given to me").
- **Self-Improvement**: A recurring theme of striving for personal growth and betterment, as seen in their reflections on past behaviors and the desire to change (e.g., "I have buckled down on my studies").

#### Concerns
- **Fear of Failure**: Writers frequently express anxiety about academic performance and the implications of not succeeding (e.g., "I’m scared that I am not going to do well here").
- **Future Uncertainty**: Concerns about future opportunities and the impact of current actions on long-term goals are prevalent (e.g., "If I can’t do that then I don’t even have to worry about any of this").
- **Social Isolation**: While they value social connections, there is often a sense of loneliness or difficulty in forming relationships, which they recognize as a barrier to their success (e.g., "I feel like I don’t know anyone").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Planning**: Writers often discuss their study habits and time management, indicating a proactive approach to their responsibilities (e.g., "I have to really sit down and study for it if I want to do well").
- **Emotional Regulation**: There is a tendency to suppress emotions or manage them internally, as seen in the mention of hiding feelings to avoid vulnerability (e.g., "I tend to do that a lot. Hide my emotions").
- **Self-Reflection**: High levels of introspection are evident, with writers frequently analyzing their thoughts, feelings, and behaviors (e.g., "I guess I shouldn’t think so hard").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: A predominance of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a personal investment in their experiences and responsibilities, reflecting a self-directed approach to their lives.
- **Collective References**: Occasionally, there are references to family and friends, highlighting the importance of social support in their conscientiousness (e.g., "my parents," "my friends").

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of HIGH Conscientiousness writers reveal a structured, achievement-oriented mindset characterized by a strong sense of responsibility, proactive planning, and a tendency to reflect deeply on their experiences and emotions. Their concerns often revolve around academic success and the implications of their actions for the future, while their writing style reflects a formal and organized approach to expressing these thoughts.

--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Conscientiousness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Informal and Disorganized**: The writing often lacks structure, with run-on sentences and frequent digressions (e.g., "I keep thinking 'compilation' for some reason...").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many examples exhibit a free-flowing thought process, jumping from one topic to another without clear transitions (e.g., "I wonder if I could be a genius writer. probably not. hmmm don't know what to write.").
- **Repetitive and Hesitant Language**: Frequent use of filler words and phrases (e.g., "I don't know," "I guess," "I wonder") indicates uncertainty and a lack of decisiveness.

#### Values and Concerns
- **Procrastination and Avoidance**: Writers express anxiety about assignments and deadlines but often delay action (e.g., "I should really be thinking about school...").
- **Low Self-Efficacy**: There is a recurring theme of self-doubt and questioning one's abilities (e.g., "I don't think I could change my mind about something...").
- **Focus on Immediate Gratification**: Interests in distractions (e.g., video games, music) often take precedence over academic responsibilities (e.g., "I got distracted and started watching him play for a minute.").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Impulsivity**: Decisions appear to be made on a whim, often without consideration of consequences (e.g., skipping responsibilities to play a show).
- **Lack of Planning**: Writers express a desire for change but do not articulate concrete steps to achieve their goals (e.g., "I need a change in my life, like college isn't a big enough change...").
- **Social Comparisons**: There is a tendency to compare oneself unfavorably to others, indicating a lack of confidence (e.g., "I am going to have like a couple of paragraphs. That is really sad.").

#### Emotional Tone
- **Anxiety and Frustration**: Many entries convey feelings of stress and overwhelm, particularly regarding academic pressures (e.g., "I am really having trouble recording my natural thought process...").
- **Discontent and Restlessness**: Writers often express dissatisfaction with their current situations, whether academic or personal (e.g., "I don't even feel at home here...").
- **Nostalgia and Regret**: There are reflections on past experiences that evoke a sense of longing or disappointment (e.g., reminiscing about family or past relationships).

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The use of "I" is prevalent, indicating a self-focused perspective that often centers on personal feelings and experiences (e.g., "I feel bad right now...").
- **Limited Use of Collective Pronouns**: There is less emphasis on group identity or collaboration, suggesting a more individualistic approach to challenges.

These patterns collectively illustrate a tendency towards disorganization, impulsivity, and a lack of proactive behavior, which are characteristic of low conscientiousness.

- HIGH Conscientiousness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Conscientiousness

#### Language Style
- **Structured and Reflective**: The writing often displays a clear structure, with coherent thoughts and logical progression. For example, the writers frequently outline their concerns and resolutions in a methodical manner.
- **Formal Vocabulary**: Use of precise language and terminology related to academic and personal goals (e.g., "assignments," "education," "success").
- **Complex Sentences**: Many sentences are longer and more complex, indicating a tendency to elaborate on thoughts and feelings, as seen in the detailed descriptions of their academic and personal lives.

#### Values
- **Achievement-Oriented**: A strong emphasis on academic success and future aspirations (e.g., "I hope to continue my studies here and then go on to Law school"). This reflects a high value placed on education and career goals.
- **Responsibility**: Writers express a sense of duty towards their studies and family, often mentioning the sacrifices made by parents and the desire to honor those sacrifices (e.g., "I’m trying not to abuse any things they had given to me").
- **Self-Improvement**: A recurring theme of striving for personal growth and betterment, as seen in their reflections on past behaviors and the desire to change (e.g., "I have buckled down on my studies").

#### Concerns
- **Fear of Failure**: Writers frequently express anxiety about academic performance and the implications of not succeeding (e.g., "I’m scared that I am not going to do well here").
- **Future Uncertainty**: Concerns about future opportunities and the impact of current actions on long-term goals are prevalent (e.g., "If I can’t do that then I don’t even have to worry about any of this").
- **Social Isolation**: While they value social connections, there is often a sense of loneliness or difficulty in forming relationships, which they recognize as a barrier to their success (e.g., "I feel like I don’t know anyone").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Proactive Planning**: Writers often discuss their study habits and time management, indicating a proactive approach to their responsibilities (e.g., "I have to really sit down and study for it if I want to do well").
- **Emotional Regulation**: There is a tendency to suppress emotions or manage them internally, as seen in the mention of hiding feelings to avoid vulnerability (e.g., "I tend to do that a lot. Hide my emotions").
- **Self-Reflection**: High levels of introspection are evident, with writers frequently analyzing their thoughts, feelings, and behaviors (e.g., "I guess I shouldn’t think so hard").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Focus**: A predominance of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a personal investment in their experiences and responsibilities, reflecting a self-directed approach to their lives.
- **Collective References**: Occasionally, there are references to family and friends, highlighting the importance of social support in their conscientiousness (e.g., "my parents," "my friends").

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of HIGH Conscientiousness writers reveal a structured, achievement-oriented mindset characterized by a strong sense of responsibility, proactive planning, and a tendency to reflect deeply on their experiences and emotions. Their concerns often revolve around academic success and the implications of their actions for the future, while their writing style reflects a formal and organized approach to expressing these thoughts.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Conscientiousness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Conscientiousness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. Language Structure: Structured and coherent writing vs informal and disorganized writing.  
2. Vocabulary Use: Formal and precise vocabulary vs casual and vague vocabulary.  
3. Emotional Tone: Reflective and introspective tone vs anxious and frustrated tone.  
4. Planning Approach: Proactive planning and time management vs impulsive and reactive behavior.  
5. Self-Reflection: High levels of introspection and analysis vs low levels of self-reflection and self-doubt.  
6. Concerns Focus: Achievement-oriented concerns about future success vs avoidance of responsibilities and procrastination.  
7. Pronoun Usage: Occasional collective references indicating social support vs frequent first-person focus indicating individualism.

What patterns characterize LOW Extraversion?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Extraversion writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Extraversion

#### Language Style
- **Simple and Direct Vocabulary**: The language tends to be straightforward, often lacking in elaborate descriptions or embellishments. For example, phrases like "I'm hungry" and "I don't like psychologists" reflect a preference for clarity over complexity.
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many entries exhibit a stream-of-consciousness style, where thoughts are presented in a flowing manner without much structure, indicating a focus on internal thoughts rather than external interactions.
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Sentences often follow a similar structure, which can create a monotonous tone. For instance, "I don't know" and "I wonder" are frequently used, suggesting uncertainty and introspection.

#### Values
- **Preference for Solitude**: There is a clear inclination towards solitude or small, familiar groups rather than large social gatherings. Writers express discomfort in social situations, as seen in comments about feeling scrutinized or uncomfortable around others.
- **Self-Reflection**: A strong focus on personal feelings and thoughts, often leading to self-criticism or doubt. Phrases like "I feel like I'm slipping" and "I don't want to disappoint" indicate a tendency to reflect deeply on personal experiences and emotions.
- **Cynicism Towards Social Norms**: Some writers express skepticism about social interactions and relationships, as seen in comments about dating and friendships, suggesting a value system that prioritizes authenticity over social acceptance.

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety and Insecurity**: Many entries reveal feelings of anxiety, such as worrying about social interactions or academic performance. Phrases like "I feel overwhelmed" and "I don't want her to be disappointed in me" highlight these concerns.
- **Fear of Judgment**: There is a recurring theme of fearing judgment from others, which can lead to avoidance of social situations. Writers often mention feeling scrutinized or uncomfortable, indicating a concern about how they are perceived.
- **Struggles with Motivation**: Writers frequently express a lack of motivation or energy, as seen in comments about feeling sick or overwhelmed, which can hinder their ability to engage socially or academically.

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Avoidance of Social Situations**: Many writers mention avoiding social gatherings or feeling uncomfortable in them, such as "I definitely do not like to be in the spotlight" or "I can never seem to get comfortable around his friends."
- **Limited Social Interaction**: There is a tendency to have few close relationships, with writers often reflecting on past friendships rather than current ones. Comments like "I have met basically zero people since I have been in college" illustrate this.
- **Focus on Internal Experiences**: Writers often describe their internal states, such as physical discomfort or emotional turmoil, rather than external events or interactions. This is evident in comments about headaches, exhaustion, and feelings of loneliness.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Melancholic and Reflective**: The overall emotional tone tends to be melancholic, with writers often reflecting on past experiences with a sense of nostalgia or regret. Phrases like "I miss my friend" and "I feel lost without it" convey this tone.
- **Self-Critical**: Many entries include self-critical remarks, indicating a lack of self-esteem or confidence. Comments like "I am such a horrible person" and "I don't think I deserve him" reflect this self-perception.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The use of "I" is prevalent, indicating a focus on personal thoughts and feelings. This suggests an inward orientation typical of low extraversion.
- **Limited Use of Second-Person Pronouns**: There is less engagement with others in the text, as seen in the infrequent use of "you" or "we," which further emphasizes the solitary nature of the writers' experiences.

These patterns collectively illustrate the characteristics of low extraversion, highlighting a preference for introspection, discomfort in social situations, and a tendency towards anxiety and self-criticism.

What patterns characterize HIGH Extraversion?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Extraversion writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Extraversion

- **Language Style:**
  - **Conversational Tone:** The use of informal language and a conversational style is prevalent. Writers often use phrases like "wow," "I guess," and "anyway," which create a casual, friendly atmosphere.
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** Many entries exhibit a free-flowing, stream-of-consciousness style, indicating a comfort with sharing thoughts and feelings openly.
  - **Repetition and Emphasis:** Frequent use of repetition (e.g., "I really," "I guess") emphasizes feelings and thoughts, showcasing enthusiasm or concern.

- **Values:**
  - **Social Connections:** A strong emphasis on relationships and social interactions is evident. Writers express a desire to connect with friends and family, highlighting the importance of these relationships in their lives.
  - **Optimism and Positivity:** Many entries reflect a hopeful outlook, with phrases like "I hope that this year will get better" and "I want to thrive here," indicating a focus on positive outcomes.
  - **Community Involvement:** Interest in joining groups or organizations (e.g., church, spirit organizations) suggests a value placed on community and shared experiences.

- **Concerns:**
  - **Social Acceptance:** Writers often express concerns about fitting in and making friends, as seen in phrases like "I hope I can get involved" and "I guess I need to give it more time."
  - **Academic Pressure:** There is a recurring theme of anxiety related to academic performance, with concerns about workload and grades, indicating a desire to succeed in a social context.
  - **Fear of Loneliness:** Many entries reflect a fear of being alone or disconnected, as seen in statements about missing friends or feeling lonely in new environments.

- **Behavioral Indicators:**
  - **Active Engagement:** Writers frequently mention activities (e.g., workouts, social events) they are looking forward to, indicating a proactive approach to socializing and engaging with their environment.
  - **Expressive Emotion:** Emotional expressions are common, with writers sharing feelings of excitement, nervousness, and longing, which suggests a high level of emotional awareness and expressiveness.
  - **Use of Personal Pronouns:** Frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a focus on personal experiences and feelings, reinforcing the individual’s connection to their social world.

- **Emotional Tone:**
  - **Enthusiastic and Hopeful:** The overall tone is often upbeat and enthusiastic, with expressions of excitement about new experiences and relationships.
  - **Vulnerability:** Writers also display vulnerability, sharing fears and uncertainties, which can foster deeper connections with others.

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that thrives on social interaction, values relationships, and expresses a range of emotions, all hallmarks of high extraversion.

distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional

  any group.

--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Extraversion patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Extraversion

#### Language Style
- **Simple and Direct Vocabulary**: The language tends to be straightforward, often lacking in elaborate descriptions or embellishments. For example, phrases like "I'm hungry" and "I don't like psychologists" reflect a preference for clarity over complexity.
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many entries exhibit a stream-of-consciousness style, where thoughts are presented in a flowing manner without much structure, indicating a focus on internal thoughts rather than external interactions.
- **Repetitive Sentence Structure**: Sentences often follow a similar structure, which can create a monotonous tone. For instance, "I don't know" and "I wonder" are frequently used, suggesting uncertainty and introspection.

#### Values
- **Preference for Solitude**: There is a clear inclination towards solitude or small, familiar groups rather than large social gatherings. Writers express discomfort in social situations, as seen in comments about feeling scrutinized or uncomfortable around others.
- **Self-Reflection**: A strong focus on personal feelings and thoughts, often leading to self-criticism or doubt. Phrases like "I feel like I'm slipping" and "I don't want to disappoint" indicate a tendency to reflect deeply on personal experiences and emotions.
- **Cynicism Towards Social Norms**: Some writers express skepticism about social interactions and relationships, as seen in comments about dating and friendships, suggesting a value system that prioritizes authenticity over social acceptance.

#### Concerns
- **Anxiety and Insecurity**: Many entries reveal feelings of anxiety, such as worrying about social interactions or academic performance. Phrases like "I feel overwhelmed" and "I don't want her to be disappointed in me" highlight these concerns.
- **Fear of Judgment**: There is a recurring theme of fearing judgment from others, which can lead to avoidance of social situations. Writers often mention feeling scrutinized or uncomfortable, indicating a concern about how they are perceived.
- **Struggles with Motivation**: Writers frequently express a lack of motivation or energy, as seen in comments about feeling sick or overwhelmed, which can hinder their ability to engage socially or academically.

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Avoidance of Social Situations**: Many writers mention avoiding social gatherings or feeling uncomfortable in them, such as "I definitely do not like to be in the spotlight" or "I can never seem to get comfortable around his friends."
- **Limited Social Interaction**: There is a tendency to have few close relationships, with writers often reflecting on past friendships rather than current ones. Comments like "I have met basically zero people since I have been in college" illustrate this.
- **Focus on Internal Experiences**: Writers often describe their internal states, such as physical discomfort or emotional turmoil, rather than external events or interactions. This is evident in comments about headaches, exhaustion, and feelings of loneliness.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Melancholic and Reflective**: The overall emotional tone tends to be melancholic, with writers often reflecting on past experiences with a sense of nostalgia or regret. Phrases like "I miss my friend" and "I feel lost without it" convey this tone.
- **Self-Critical**: Many entries include self-critical remarks, indicating a lack of self-esteem or confidence. Comments like "I am such a horrible person" and "I don't think I deserve him" reflect this self-perception.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The use of "I" is prevalent, indicating a focus on personal thoughts and feelings. This suggests an inward orientation typical of low extraversion.
- **Limited Use of Second-Person Pronouns**: There is less engagement with others in the text, as seen in the infrequent use of "you" or "we," which further emphasizes the solitary nature of the writers' experiences.

These patterns collectively illustrate the characteristics of low extraversion, highlighting a preference for introspection, discomfort in social situations, and a tendency towards anxiety and self-criticism.

- HIGH Extraversion patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Extraversion

- **Language Style:**
  - **Conversational Tone:** The use of informal language and a conversational style is prevalent. Writers often use phrases like "wow," "I guess," and "anyway," which create a casual, friendly atmosphere.
  - **Stream of Consciousness:** Many entries exhibit a free-flowing, stream-of-consciousness style, indicating a comfort with sharing thoughts and feelings openly.
  - **Repetition and Emphasis:** Frequent use of repetition (e.g., "I really," "I guess") emphasizes feelings and thoughts, showcasing enthusiasm or concern.

  - **Optimism and Positivity:** Many entries reflect a hopeful outlook, with phrases like "I hope that this year will get better" and "I want to thrive here," indicating a focus on positive outcomes.
  - **Community Involvement:** Interest in joining groups or organizations (e.g., church, spirit organizations) suggests a value placed on community and shared experiences.

  - **Social Acceptance:** Writers often express concerns about fitting in and making friends, as seen in phrases like "I hope I can get involved" and "I guess I need to give it more time."
  - **Academic Pressure:** There is a recurring theme of anxiety related to academic performance, with concerns about workload and grades, indicating a desire to succeed in a social context.
  - **Fear of Loneliness:** Many entries reflect a fear of being alone or disconnected, as seen in statements about missing friends or feeling lonely in new environments.

  - **Active Engagement:** Writers frequently mention activities (e.g., workouts, social events) they are looking forward to, indicating a proactive approach to socializing and engaging with their environment.

  - **Use of Personal Pronouns:** Frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a focus on personal experiences and feelings, reinforcing the individual’s connection to their social world.

  - **Enthusiastic and Hopeful:** The overall tone is often upbeat and enthusiastic, with expressions of excitement about new experiences and relationships.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Extraversion, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Extraversion when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. Language Tone: Conversational and informal vs. simple and direct.  
2. Emotional Expression: Enthusiastic and hopeful vs. melancholic and self-critical.  
3. Social Focus: Emphasis on relationships and community vs. preference for solitude and introspection.  
4. Concerns: Worries about fitting in and social acceptance vs. fears of judgment and social scrutiny.  
5. Engagement Level: Active participation in social activities vs. avoidance of social situations.  
6. Pronoun Usage: Frequent use of first-person and inclusive pronouns vs. predominant use of first-person singular pronouns.  
7. Thought Structure: Free-flowing and expressive vs. repetitive and structured.

What patterns characterize LOW Agreeableness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Agreeableness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Direct and Blunt Language**: The writers often use straightforward, unembellished language, indicating a lack of concern for politeness or social niceties. For example, phrases like "I don't liberals" and "the cooks here should go down in the Guinness Book of World Records for being the first people ever to make chicken taste bad" reflect a candid, sometimes harsh tone.
- **Colloquial and Informal Tone**: The use of slang and casual expressions (e.g., "Duhhh," "crap," "hogwash") suggests a relaxed attitude towards language, often prioritizing personal expression over formality.

#### Values
- **Individualism**: There is a strong emphasis on personal beliefs and opinions, often dismissing collective or societal norms. For instance, the writer critiques liberal views on gun control, showcasing a preference for personal freedom over communal safety.
- **Skepticism Towards Authority**: Many writers express distrust towards institutions or figures of authority, such as teachers or government regulations, indicating a tendency to question established norms.

#### Concerns
- **Self-Centered Focus**: The narratives often center around personal experiences and feelings, with little regard for others' perspectives. For example, concerns about academic performance or personal relationships are framed primarily in terms of how they affect the writer.
- **Frustration and Discontent**: Many entries convey dissatisfaction with various aspects of life, such as living conditions, food quality, or social interactions, often expressed through complaints or negative observations.

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Conflictual Interactions**: Writers frequently describe arguments or disagreements, indicating a tendency to engage in confrontational behavior. For example, one writer discusses a fight with a girlfriend, highlighting a short temper and difficulty in resolving conflicts amicably.
- **Social Withdrawal**: There are indications of reluctance to engage with others, as seen in the mention of feeling isolated or having difficulty making friends. This is coupled with a tendency to blame external factors for social challenges, such as living arrangements or time constraints.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynicism and Sarcasm**: The emotional tone often leans towards cynicism, with sarcastic remarks about societal issues or personal experiences. This is evident in the dismissal of others' opinions or the trivialization of serious topics.
- **Anxiety and Frustration**: Many entries reflect feelings of anxiety or frustration, particularly regarding academic pressures or social dynamics, suggesting a struggle with emotional regulation.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The predominant use of "I" and "my" indicates a self-focused perspective, reinforcing the individualistic nature of the narratives. This focus on the self often overshadows the experiences or feelings of others.

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of individuals with low agreeableness are characterized by directness, individualism, skepticism, and a focus on personal grievances. Their emotional tone often reflects cynicism and frustration, with a tendency to engage in conflict and withdraw socially.

What patterns characterize HIGH Agreeableness?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Agreeableness writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Positive Emotional Tone**: Writers frequently express gratitude, affection, and optimism (e.g., "She's so sweet," "I had a good time," "I love everyone and everything in this world").
- **Conversational and Informal**: The use of casual language and stream-of-consciousness writing reflects a relaxed and friendly tone (e.g., "oops," "gosh," "oh well").
- **Use of Exclamations**: Frequent use of exclamation marks conveys enthusiasm and excitement (e.g., "Go Ricki!," "Thank goodness!").

#### Values
- **Interpersonal Relationships**: A strong emphasis on friendships and connections (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I want to be successful at college: academically, spiritually, emotionally and physically").
- **Empathy and Compassion**: Expressions of concern for others' feelings and situations (e.g., "I always feel bad for the customers," "I hope things will get better for her").
- **Desire for Harmony**: A tendency to avoid conflict and seek consensus (e.g., "I can forgive and forget," "I want to feel like a part of things").

#### Concerns
- **Self-Reflection**: Writers often ponder their own feelings and experiences, indicating a desire for personal growth and understanding (e.g., "I guess that my twenty minutes is up," "I want to be successful at college").
- **Fear of Judgment**: Concerns about how others perceive them and a desire to fit in (e.g., "I feel like I am too real for everyone I have met," "I hope they don't think I'm a dumbass").
- **Balancing Priorities**: Writers express struggles with managing time and responsibilities, often prioritizing social interactions over academic tasks (e.g., "I guess I’ll just walk back home... it is good exercise for me").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Social Engagement**: Frequent mentions of social activities and interactions with friends (e.g., "I want to invite Matt over again," "I am going to be late for practice").
- **Supportive Actions**: Descriptions of helping others or being there for friends (e.g., "I go for moral support and to help out," "I hope he knows how to change a flat").
- **Adaptability**: Willingness to adjust plans and be flexible in social situations (e.g., "I can always drive back in time," "I guess I can forgive and forget").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Inclusive Language**: Frequent use of "we," "us," and "our" suggests a focus on community and shared experiences (e.g., "We have a floor meeting tonight," "I want one of our good friends from Chicago to come").
- **Self-Referencing**: Use of "I" indicates personal reflection and a focus on individual feelings, but often in relation to others (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I want to tell him how important he is to me").

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that values connection, empathy, and a positive outlook, characteristic of high agreeableness.

[Record number Agreeableness-turn3-synthesis]

--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Agreeableness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Direct and Blunt Language**: The writers often use straightforward, unembellished language, indicating a lack of concern for politeness or social niceties. For example, phrases like "I don't liberals" and "the cooks here should go down in the Guinness Book of World Records for being the first people ever to make chicken taste bad" reflect a candid, sometimes harsh tone.
- **Colloquial and Informal Tone**: The use of slang and casual expressions (e.g., "Duhhh," "crap," "hogwash") suggests a relaxed attitude towards language, often prioritizing personal expression over formality.

#### Values
- **Individualism**: There is a strong emphasis on personal beliefs and opinions, often dismissing collective or societal norms. For instance, the writer critiques liberal views on gun control, showcasing a preference for personal freedom over communal safety.
- **Skepticism Towards Authority**: Many writers express distrust towards institutions or figures of authority, such as teachers or government regulations, indicating a tendency to question established norms.

#### Concerns
- **Self-Centered Focus**: The narratives often center around personal experiences and feelings, with little regard for others' perspectives. For example, concerns about academic performance or personal relationships are framed primarily in terms of how they affect the writer.
- **Frustration and Discontent**: Many entries convey dissatisfaction with various aspects of life, such as living conditions, food quality, or social interactions, often expressed through complaints or negative observations.

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Conflictual Interactions**: Writers frequently describe arguments or disagreements, indicating a tendency to engage in confrontational behavior. For example, one writer discusses a fight with a girlfriend, highlighting a short temper and difficulty in resolving conflicts amicably.
- **Social Withdrawal**: There are indications of reluctance to engage with others, as seen in the mention of feeling isolated or having difficulty making friends. This is coupled with a tendency to blame external factors for social challenges, such as living arrangements or time constraints.

#### Emotional Tone
- **Cynicism and Sarcasm**: The emotional tone often leans towards cynicism, with sarcastic remarks about societal issues or personal experiences. This is evident in the dismissal of others' opinions or the trivialization of serious topics.
- **Anxiety and Frustration**: Many entries reflect feelings of anxiety or frustration, particularly regarding academic pressures or social dynamics, suggesting a struggle with emotional regulation.

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The predominant use of "I" and "my" indicates a self-focused perspective, reinforcing the individualistic nature of the narratives. This focus on the self often overshadows the experiences or feelings of others.

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of individuals with low agreeableness are characterized by directness, individualism, skepticism, and a focus on personal grievances. Their emotional tone often reflects cynicism and frustration, with a tendency to engage in conflict and withdraw socially.

- HIGH Agreeableness patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Agreeableness

#### Language Style
- **Positive Emotional Tone**: Writers frequently express gratitude, affection, and optimism (e.g., "She's so sweet," "I had a good time," "I love everyone and everything in this world").
- **Conversational and Informal**: The use of casual language and stream-of-consciousness writing reflects a relaxed and friendly tone (e.g., "oops," "gosh," "oh well").
- **Use of Exclamations**: Frequent use of exclamation marks conveys enthusiasm and excitement (e.g., "Go Ricki!," "Thank goodness!").

#### Values
- **Interpersonal Relationships**: A strong emphasis on friendships and connections (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I want to be successful at college: academically, spiritually, emotionally and physically").
- **Empathy and Compassion**: Expressions of concern for others' feelings and situations (e.g., "I always feel bad for the customers," "I hope things will get better for her").
- **Desire for Harmony**: A tendency to avoid conflict and seek consensus (e.g., "I can forgive and forget," "I want to feel like a part of things").

#### Concerns
- **Self-Reflection**: Writers often ponder their own feelings and experiences, indicating a desire for personal growth and understanding (e.g., "I guess that my twenty minutes is up," "I want to be successful at college").
- **Fear of Judgment**: Concerns about how others perceive them and a desire to fit in (e.g., "I feel like I am too real for everyone I have met," "I hope they don't think I'm a dumbass").
- **Balancing Priorities**: Writers express struggles with managing time and responsibilities, often prioritizing social interactions over academic tasks (e.g., "I guess I’ll just walk back home... it is good exercise for me").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Social Engagement**: Frequent mentions of social activities and interactions with friends (e.g., "I want to invite Matt over again," "I am going to be late for practice").
- **Supportive Actions**: Descriptions of helping others or being there for friends (e.g., "I go for moral support and to help out," "I hope he knows how to change a flat").
- **Adaptability**: Willingness to adjust plans and be flexible in social situations (e.g., "I can always drive back in time," "I guess I can forgive and forget").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **Inclusive Language**: Frequent use of "we," "us," and "our" suggests a focus on community and shared experiences (e.g., "We have a floor meeting tonight," "I want one of our good friends from Chicago to come").
- **Self-Referencing**: Use of "I" indicates personal reflection and a focus on individual feelings, but often in relation to others (e.g., "I miss a lot of people," "I want to tell him how important he is to me").

These patterns collectively illustrate a personality that values connection, empathy, and a positive outlook, characteristic of high agreeableness.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Agreeableness, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Agreeableness when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. **Emotional Tone**: Positive expressions of gratitude and affection vs. cynical and sarcastic remarks.  
2. **Language Style**: Use of casual, friendly language with exclamations vs. direct, blunt language with informal slang.  
3. **Focus on Relationships**: Emphasis on interpersonal connections and support vs. self-centered focus on personal grievances.  
4. **Conflict Approach**: Desire for harmony and consensus vs. tendency to engage in confrontational behavior.  
5. **Self-Reflection**: Pondering personal growth and understanding vs. expressing frustration and dissatisfaction.  
6. **Social Engagement**: Frequent mentions of social activities and interactions vs. indications of social withdrawal and isolation.  
7. **Pronoun Usage**: Inclusive language with "we" and "us" vs. predominant use of first-person pronouns "I" and "my."

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

What patterns characterize LOW Neuroticism?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these LOW Neuroticism writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Optimistic Tone**: The writers often express a positive outlook on life, focusing on potential and opportunities rather than problems. For example, phrases like "I am so blessed" and "I am incredibly pleased" indicate a positive emotional state.
- **Conversational and Casual**: The use of informal language and a conversational tone (e.g., "oh my goodness," "damnit") suggests comfort and ease in expressing thoughts.
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many entries exhibit a free-flowing style, where thoughts are expressed as they come, indicating a relaxed mental state. This is evident in the long, unstructured sentences and the use of ellipses.

#### Values
- **Faith and Spirituality**: A recurring theme is the importance of faith and moral values, as seen in discussions about God and personal beliefs. Writers often reflect on how their faith influences their actions and decisions.
- **Friendship and Relationships**: There is a strong emphasis on the value of friendships and connections with others, highlighting the importance of social bonds (e.g., "I feel so connected to somebody").
- **Personal Growth**: Writers express a desire for self-improvement and learning, often reflecting on past experiences and how they have shaped their current perspectives.

#### Concerns
- **Future Orientation**: Writers frequently contemplate their future, expressing curiosity and excitement about what lies ahead (e.g., "What am I going to major in?"). This indicates a proactive approach to life.
- **Self-Reflection**: There is a tendency to engage in self-reflection, questioning personal choices and behaviors, but in a constructive manner (e.g., "I wonder why I went against my beliefs").
- **Social Dynamics**: Concerns about social interactions and relationships are present, but they are approached with a sense of humor or light-heartedness (e.g., "I hate boys but I love them").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Active Engagement**: Writers describe participating in social activities (e.g., parties, group outings) and express enjoyment in these experiences, indicating a willingness to engage with the world.
- **Decision-Making**: There is a clear sense of agency in decision-making, as seen in statements about choosing not to drink or prioritizing friendships over romantic relationships.
- **Adaptability**: Writers demonstrate flexibility in their thoughts and actions, often adjusting their plans based on circumstances (e.g., "I think I will go to the pool instead").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Perspective**: The frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a focus on personal experiences and feelings, suggesting a strong sense of self.
- **Inclusive Language**: The use of "we" and "us" in social contexts reflects a sense of community and connection with others, reinforcing the importance of relationships.

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of individuals with low neuroticism are characterized by optimism, a focus on relationships and personal growth, and a proactive approach to life. Their writing reflects a relaxed and open mindset, with an emphasis on faith, social connections, and adaptability in the face of challenges.
================================================================================

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.

9. Towers seemed to have gotten an illness that spread very quickly last week. Only difference is everyone felt like crap for a day maybe two, I was sick for four. What patterns characterize HIGH Neuroticism?
Focus on: language style, values, concerns, behavioral indicators.

Provide a structured description of the patterns you observe (bullet
points are fine). Be specific and reference the kind of cues
(vocabulary, sentence structure, topics, emotional tone, pronouns, etc.)
that recur across these HIGH Neuroticism writers.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The writers often use "I" and "my," indicating a self-focused perspective. This suggests a preoccupation with personal feelings and experiences (e.g., "I feel worthless," "I want to be involved").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing style is often disjointed and chaotic, reflecting racing thoughts and a lack of organization (e.g., rapid topic shifts, incomplete sentences).
- **Repetitive Language**: Phrases like "I really want," "I need," and "I guess" are common, indicating uncertainty and a desire for reassurance.

#### Emotional Tone
- **High Levels of Anxiety and Worry**: Writers express concerns about academic performance, social relationships, and personal well-being (e.g., "I am scared that everyone is mad," "I feel so awful that I cry").
- **Negative Self-Perception**: There is a tendency to express feelings of inadequacy and self-doubt (e.g., "I feel worthless," "I don't think that I have any chance of finishing college").
- **Frustration and Irritability**: Many entries convey irritation with external circumstances or people (e.g., "It pisses me off that ACC's records department is screwed up," "I hate lawyers they're scum").

#### Values and Concerns
- **Concern for Social Acceptance**: Writers often reflect on their relationships and how they are perceived by others (e.g., "I hope she doesn't find out I am writing about her," "I wonder if anyone is going to be able to read my typing").
- **Desire for Independence**: There is a recurring theme of wanting freedom from parental control and societal expectations (e.g., "I can't wait to get out of here and get my own place," "I like being independent").
- **Struggle with Identity and Purpose**: Writers frequently question their life choices and future paths, indicating existential concerns (e.g., "I want to do something that I will enjoy," "I wonder if I am wasting my life away").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination and Overcommitment**: Many entries reveal a tendency to take on too much and then feel overwhelmed (e.g., "I am committing to too many activities," "I have so much homework to do").
- **Physical Symptoms of Stress**: Writers often mention physical discomforts linked to stress, such as headaches or fatigue (e.g., "I am extremely tired," "My neck really hurts").
- **Avoidance of Social Situations**: There is a tendency to avoid social interactions or express discomfort in social settings (e.g., "I am scared though to walk on the strip by myself," "I don't know anyone at this school").

### Summary
Overall, the patterns observed in HIGH Neuroticism writers reflect a preoccupation with self, a tendency toward anxiety and negative emotions, and a struggle with identity and social acceptance. Their writing often conveys a chaotic thought process, revealing deep-seated concerns about their academic and social lives, as well as a desire for independence and self-fulfillment.
================================================================================

--- SYSTEM PROMPT ---

You are an expert in personality psychology, psycholinguistics, and
qualitative text analysis.

You will help discover the linguistic and behavioural patterns that
distinguish HIGH vs LOW levels of a single Big Five personality trait
through a short multi-turn dialogue.

Guidelines:
- Reason carefully and ground every claim in the provided texts.
- Focus on language style, recurring topics, values, concerns, emotional
  tone, pronoun usage, and behavioural indicators.
- Be concise but specific. Avoid generic statements that could apply to
  any group.
- Do not invent evidence that is not present in the texts.


--- USER PROMPT ---

Summary so far:

- LOW Neuroticism patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing LOW Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Optimistic Tone**: The writers often express a positive outlook on life, focusing on potential and opportunities rather than problems. For example, phrases like "I am so blessed" and "I am incredibly pleased" indicate a positive emotional state.
- **Conversational and Casual**: The use of informal language and a conversational tone (e.g., "oh my goodness," "damnit") suggests comfort and ease in expressing thoughts.
- **Stream of Consciousness**: Many entries exhibit a free-flowing style, where thoughts are expressed as they come, indicating a relaxed mental state. This is evident in the long, unstructured sentences and the use of ellipses.

#### Values
- **Faith and Spirituality**: A recurring theme is the importance of faith and moral values, as seen in discussions about God and personal beliefs. Writers often reflect on how their faith influences their actions and decisions.
- **Friendship and Relationships**: There is a strong emphasis on the value of friendships and connections with others, highlighting the importance of social bonds (e.g., "I feel so connected to somebody").
- **Personal Growth**: Writers express a desire for self-improvement and learning, often reflecting on past experiences and how they have shaped their current perspectives.

#### Concerns
- **Future Orientation**: Writers frequently contemplate their future, expressing curiosity and excitement about what lies ahead (e.g., "What am I going to major in?"). This indicates a proactive approach to life.
- **Self-Reflection**: There is a tendency to engage in self-reflection, questioning personal choices and behaviors, but in a constructive manner (e.g., "I wonder why I went against my beliefs").
- **Social Dynamics**: Concerns about social interactions and relationships are present, but they are approached with a sense of humor or light-heartedness (e.g., "I hate boys but I love them").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Active Engagement**: Writers describe participating in social activities (e.g., parties, group outings) and express enjoyment in these experiences, indicating a willingness to engage with the world.
- **Decision-Making**: There is a clear sense of agency in decision-making, as seen in statements about choosing not to drink or prioritizing friendships over romantic relationships.
- **Adaptability**: Writers demonstrate flexibility in their thoughts and actions, often adjusting their plans based on circumstances (e.g., "I think I will go to the pool instead").

#### Pronoun Usage
- **First-Person Perspective**: The frequent use of first-person pronouns ("I," "my") indicates a focus on personal experiences and feelings, suggesting a strong sense of self.
- **Inclusive Language**: The use of "we" and "us" in social contexts reflects a sense of community and connection with others, reinforcing the importance of relationships.

### Summary
Overall, the linguistic and behavioral patterns of individuals with low neuroticism are characterized by optimism, a focus on relationships and personal growth, and a proactive approach to life. Their writing reflects a relaxed and open mindset, with an emphasis on faith, social connections, and adaptability in the face of challenges.

- HIGH Neuroticism patterns:
### Patterns Characterizing HIGH Neuroticism

#### Language Style
- **Frequent Use of First-Person Pronouns**: The writers often use "I" and "my," indicating a self-focused perspective. This suggests a preoccupation with personal feelings and experiences (e.g., "I feel worthless," "I want to be involved").
- **Stream of Consciousness**: The writing style is often disjointed and chaotic, reflecting racing thoughts and a lack of organization (e.g., rapid topic shifts, incomplete sentences).
- **Repetitive Language**: Phrases like "I really want," "I need," and "I guess" are common, indicating uncertainty and a desire for reassurance.

#### Emotional Tone
- **High Levels of Anxiety and Worry**: Writers express concerns about academic performance, social relationships, and personal well-being (e.g., "I am scared that everyone is mad," "I feel so awful that I cry").
- **Negative Self-Perception**: There is a tendency to express feelings of inadequacy and self-doubt (e.g., "I feel worthless," "I don't think that I have any chance of finishing college").
- **Frustration and Irritability**: Many entries convey irritation with external circumstances or people (e.g., "It pisses me off that ACC's records department is screwed up," "I hate lawyers they're scum").

#### Values and Concerns
- **Concern for Social Acceptance**: Writers often reflect on their relationships and how they are perceived by others (e.g., "I hope she doesn't find out I am writing about her," "I wonder if anyone is going to be able to read my typing").
- **Desire for Independence**: There is a recurring theme of wanting freedom from parental control and societal expectations (e.g., "I can't wait to get out of here and get my own place," "I like being independent").
- **Struggle with Identity and Purpose**: Writers frequently question their life choices and future paths, indicating existential concerns (e.g., "I want to do something that I will enjoy," "I wonder if I am wasting my life away").

#### Behavioral Indicators
- **Procrastination and Overcommitment**: Many entries reveal a tendency to take on too much and then feel overwhelmed (e.g., "I am committing to too many activities," "I have so much homework to do").
- **Physical Symptoms of Stress**: Writers often mention physical discomforts linked to stress, such as headaches or fatigue (e.g., "I am extremely tired," "My neck really hurts").
- **Avoidance of Social Situations**: There is a tendency to avoid social interactions or express discomfort in social settings (e.g., "I am scared though to walk on the strip by myself," "I don't know anyone at this school").

### Summary
Overall, the patterns observed in HIGH Neuroticism writers reflect a preoccupation with self, a tendency toward anxiety and negative emotions, and a struggle with identity and social acceptance. Their writing often conveys a chaotic thought process, revealing deep-seated concerns about their academic and social lives, as well as a desire for independence and self-fulfillment.

Based on the contrast between LOW and HIGH Neuroticism, extract 5 to 7
clear, actionable principles that can be used to distinguish HIGH from
LOW Neuroticism when reading a new text.

Each principle must be a single line in EXACTLY this format:

[Principle name]: [HIGH description] vs [LOW description]

Requirements:
- Number the principles 1. 2. 3. ...
- Each principle should capture ONE concrete, observable contrast
  (lexical, stylistic, topical, or behavioural).
- Keep descriptions short (one clause each) and parallel in structure.
- Do not add commentary, headers, or explanations outside the numbered
  list.


--- LLM RESPONSE ---
1. **Pronoun Usage**: HIGH writers frequently use "I" and "my" to indicate self-focus vs LOW writers use inclusive language like "we" and "us" to emphasize community.  
2. **Emotional Tone**: HIGH writers express anxiety and negative self-perception vs LOW writers convey optimism and positive outlooks.  
3. **Language Style**: HIGH writers exhibit disjointed and chaotic writing vs LOW writers maintain a conversational and fluid style.  
4. **Concerns**: HIGH writers focus on social acceptance and identity struggles vs LOW writers emphasize personal growth and future opportunities.  
5. **Behavioral Indicators**: HIGH writers show signs of procrastination and avoidance vs LOW writers demonstrate active engagement and adaptability.  
6. **Repetitive Language**: HIGH writers use repetitive phrases indicating uncertainty vs LOW writers express confidence and decisiveness in their statements.  
7. **Self-Reflection**: HIGH writers question their choices with self-doubt vs LOW writers reflect constructively on their experiences and decisions.
